User: Given are synthetic videos of three synchronous cameras viewing simulated mice from top, front and side. A DeepLabCut-trained method is used to extract body parts per frame, and therewith per triplet of video frames. The recorded data can be used for a 3D reconstruction via bundle adjustment. If there are at least 2 rays from two images a 3D body parts position can be computed for time t = frame i. In addition, camera exterior and interior parameters can be input as well as estimated.

As body parts can be occluded or detections can be missing, not all body parts at times t can be 3D reconstructed.

In the uploaded conceptual paper we suggest to overcome missing part detections by introducing a path constraint combining a rigid or dynamic mouse model with the bundle adjustment as another constraint in addition to the observation equations of the image coordinates.

Assistant: Yes — this is a very good formulation, and the idea is sound.

What you are describing is essentially a **temporally regularized, model-based bundle adjustment** where missing 2D detections are handled not by imputation first, but by **joint estimation** under (i) image reprojection constraints and (ii) mouse motion/shape constraints. That matches the direction in your conceptual paper (motion-track constraint, rigid/deformable mouse model, least-squares adjustment, and Ceres feasibility). citeturn6view0turn2view0

## Core concept: turn missing detections into latent variables, not preprocessing gaps

Instead of filling missing DeepLabCut detections before optimization, treat them as *missing observations* and optimize a state trajectory that is still constrained by:

- available image measurements,
- camera geometry,
- mouse kinematics / rigid structure,
- temporal smoothness / path model,
- optional learned deformable prior.

That way, frames/body parts with no 2-view triangulation can still be estimated from neighboring times and the body model.

---

## Recommended state parameterization (important design choice)

There are two useful formulations.

### A) Classical BA-like formulation (explicit 3D body-part points)
Unknowns:
- camera intrinsics/extrinsics (fixed or estimated),
- per-frame 3D body-part positions \(X_{j,t}\),
- optionally per-frame mouse pose \(T_t\) and deformation variables.

This looks most like standard BA and is easy to conceptualize.

### B) Model-based trajectory formulation (recommended for your case)
Unknowns:
- static camera parameters (or subset to refine),
- per-frame mouse pose \(T_t \in SE(3)\) (translation + rotation),
- per-frame deformation state \(z_t\) (or low-dimensional offsets),
- maybe a few global mouse shape parameters.

Then 3D body-part positions are *derived*:
- rigid: \(X_{j,t} = T_t \, B_j\)
- deformable: \(X_{j,t} = T_t \, (B_j + \Delta_j(z_t))\)

This reduces dimensionality and naturally enforces structure. It also avoids optimizing many free 3D points that can drift when observations are sparse.

**My suggestion:** start with **B (rigid)**, then extend to **B (deformable)**.

---

## Objective function as a sum of residual blocks

Formulate the problem as weighted nonlinear least squares:

\[
\min \sum r_{\text{obs}} + \sum r_{\text{path}} + \sum r_{\text{rigid/deform}} + \sum r_{\text{priors}}
\]

### 1) Reprojection residuals (observation equations)
For each available detection \((c,j,t)\):
- camera \(c\), body part \(j\), time \(t\)
- observed 2D point \(u_{cjt}\)

Residual:
- projected model point minus observed image point.

Use:
- DeepLabCut confidence as weight,
- robust loss (Huber/Cauchy) to suppress misdetections/outliers.

If a detection is missing: **no residual block is added** (not a zero value).

---

### 2) Path / motion-track constraint (your key innovation)
This is the crucial part.

You can implement it as a **soft constraint** between neighboring frames (or via spline control points), e.g.:

- **constant velocity prior** on translation and rotation,
- **constant acceleration prior** (second-order smoothness),
- **cubic spline prior** (as in your concept) on pose parameters,
- or a learned motion prior residual.

Examples:
- translation smoothness: \(p_{t+1} - 2p_t + p_{t-1}\)
- rotation smoothness in Lie algebra (do not smooth Euler angles directly if possible)
- spline consistency residuals between discrete poses and spline-evaluated poses.

This lets frames with insufficient rays “borrow strength” from adjacent frames.

**Important:** for rotations, prefer **SE(3)/quaternion manifold updates** + Lie algebra residuals over independent angle interpolation if implementation effort allows. It is more stable.

---

### 3) Rigid-model constraint
For rigid body landmarks (ears, nose triangle, torso anchors, etc.), constrain relative geometry:

- fixed distances (bone lengths / landmark distances),
- fixed relative coordinates in mouse frame,
- optional body axis orthogonality/pose constraints.

This prevents collapse when observations are weak.

You can impose these either by:
- parameterization (best; no residual needed because geometry is built in), or
- penalty residuals on pairwise distances.

---

### 4) Deformable-model / learned prior constraint
Once rigid works, add deformability in a controlled way.

Two practical routes:

#### (i) Low-dimensional deformation basis (first practical extension)
Let each frame have latent \(z_t\), and body-part offsets are:
- \(\Delta(z_t)\) from PCA/autoencoder/basis model.

Add priors:
- \(\|z_t\|^2\) (regularization),
- temporal smoothness on \(z_t\),
- optional coupling to gait phase/contact state.

#### (ii) Deep-learned dynamics prior (your paper direction)
Use a network (Transformer/LSTM) to predict expected deformable positions or latent state from context.

Then add pseudo-observation residuals such as:
- predicted body-part image positions vs estimated ones, or
- predicted 3D offsets vs estimated offsets.

This should be **softly weighted**, not hard-enforced, so the geometry can override bad predictions.

Your paper explicitly frames deep-learned deformable motion plus motion-track smoothness within least-squares adjustment, and notes Ceres as a feasible implementation path. citeturn6view0

---

## How missing detections are handled in practice

You correctly note that not all body parts at all times are reconstructable from 2 rays.

With the proposed joint optimization:
- if a part is seen in 2–3 views: geometry dominates;
- if seen in 1 view: path/model constraints + single-view reprojection can still localize plausibly;
- if seen in 0 views: estimate comes only from temporal/model priors (lower confidence).

This suggests you should output not only trajectories but also **quality/confidence flags**:
- observation-supported,
- prior-supported,
- extrapolated/interpolated.

That matters later for behavior analysis.

---

## Camera parameters: fixed vs estimated (and identifiability)

You mention camera exterior/interior parameters can be input or estimated.

### Recommended strategy
1. **Fix cameras first** (from calibration / lock-box control points).
2. Validate the mouse trajectory pipeline.
3. Only then allow **small refinement** of selected camera parameters with strong priors.

Why:
- joint camera + animal + deformable dynamics estimation can become weakly constrained,
- gauge freedoms and correlations increase quickly,
- missing observations worsen identifiability.

If you refine cameras, anchor the global frame:
- fix one camera pose (or fix lock-box landmarks),
- constrain scale (if not already metric),
- add priors on intrinsics/extrinsics.

---

## Practical optimization strategy (implementation concept)

I’d implement this in **stages**, not one giant solve from day one.

### Stage 1 — Geometric baseline
- known cameras fixed
- rigid mouse model
- reprojection + path smoothness only
- initialize pose per frame from triangulatable landmarks (>=2 views)
- interpolate gaps for initialization

### Stage 2 — Robustification
- add robust losses
- DLC confidence weighting
- outlier rejection/gating (epipolar / temporal sanity checks)

### Stage 3 — Deformable extension
- low-dimensional deformable state \(z_t\)
- temporal prior on \(z_t\)
- optional contact/ground-plane priors

### Stage 4 — Learned prior integration
- network prediction as soft residuals
- tune weights carefully (anneal from stronger prior to weaker prior, or vice versa depending stability)

### Stage 5 — Optional camera refinement
- small subset of intrinsics/extrinsics
- strong priors
- only after everything else is stable

A **sliding-window** version is often easier to stabilize/debug first, then move to full-sequence batch.

---

## Ceres vs Python implementation (discussion, no code)

## Why Ceres is a strong fit here
Ceres is specifically designed for nonlinear least squares, has extensive BA support, robust losses, automatic differentiation, and manifold support (useful for rotations/quaternions). Its documentation also explicitly highlights BA structure and Schur-based solvers for efficient large-scale bundle adjustment. citeturn10view0turn10view1

### Ceres advantages for your problem
- **Natural residual-block modeling** (one block per observation / path prior / model prior)
- **Automatic differentiation** for complex reprojection + deformable residuals
- **Robust losses** for imperfect DLC detections
- **Manifolds** for rotation / pose parameterization
- **Scalability** for large sparse problems
- Well-suited to **batch least-squares adjustment**, which matches your paper’s framing. citeturn10view0turn10view1turn6view0

### Ceres caveats
- C++ development overhead is higher initially
- Deep-learning model integration is less convenient than in Python (unless you precompute predictions)
- True hard constraints/inequalities are not its main style (best handled by parameterization or soft residuals)

---

## What “Python implementation” can mean (important distinction)

There are **three different Python routes**, and they are not equal:

### 1) Pure Python + SciPy (good for prototyping)
Good for:
- proof of concept,
- testing residual definitions,
- small synthetic sequences,
- sanity checks against known ground truth.

Weaknesses:
- limited BA-specific sparse/block exploitation compared to Ceres,
- manifold handling is more manual,
- can become slow/fragile for long sequences + many landmarks + deformable states.

### 2) Python front-end + Ceres backend (very attractive hybrid)
This is often the sweet spot:
- data handling, experiments, visualization in Python
- optimization core in Ceres (C++ or Python bindings)

There are now Python bindings/projects (e.g., **pyceres**) that expose Ceres-based factor-graph/BA workflows, with packages distributed via PyPI/conda in recent releases. citeturn11search0turn11search1turn11search3

### 3) Python autodiff stack (JAX/PyTorch) for end-to-end learning + optimization
Good if:
- you want tight integration of learned deformation priors,
- differentiable training through optimization is a goal.

But:
- reproducing BA-grade sparse second-order solvers and manifold handling is substantial work,
- easy to get something working, harder to get it numerically robust and efficient.

---

## My recommendation for your project (conceptually)

Given your setup (multi-view geometry + missing detections + path constraint + potential learned deformable model), I would choose:

### **Recommended architecture**
- **Ceres for the optimization core**
- **Python for data pipeline / experiment orchestration / visualization**
- Start rigid, then deformable, then learned prior

This gives you:
- scientific clarity (clean residual design),
- performance and stability,
- easy experimentation in Python,
- a path to publication-quality implementation.

---

## A subtle but very important design choice

You called it “bundle adjustment with path constraint,” which is conceptually right, but in implementation you should decide early:

- **Do you optimize per-frame body-part 3D points explicitly?** (classical BA flavor)
- **Or do you optimize pose + deformation latent variables and derive body points?** (model-based tracking flavor)

For your use case, the second is usually better because the missing-data problem is primarily a **state estimation problem with geometric observations**, not just a sparse SfM problem.

Assistant: Great — with **B) model-based trajectory** you’re essentially doing *constrained / regularized bundle adjustment*: same reprojection observation model, but with extra (soft) constraints in the least-squares objective. That’s absolutely still “BA” in the broad photogrammetry sense (BA + constraints/priors). citeturn0search1turn0search7

Below is a concrete implementation concept that matches your stages, uses a **Python front-end + Ceres back-end**, and starts with a **rigid mouse model + path constraint**, with a clean upgrade path to deformable later.

---

## 1) Variables (parameter blocks)

### Per-frame mouse state (primary unknowns)
For each frame \(t\):
- **Pose** \(T_t \in SE(3)\) (translation + rotation)
  - rotation as quaternion with manifold/local parameterization (recommended)
  - translation as 3-vector

This is the “trajectory” you optimize.

### Rigid mouse template (fixed for stage 1)
- A set of canonical body-part coordinates \(B_j\) in mouse-local coordinates (rigid).
- Then the predicted 3D point is:
  \[
  X_{j,t} = T_t \, B_j
  \]
No per-frame 3D points are optimized; they’re derived.

### Cameras
You want:
- default: cameras fixed (intrinsics/extrinsics known)
- optional: estimate/refine cameras later

So define camera parameter blocks that can be:
- held constant, or
- enabled for optimization with strong priors

Camera blocks could include:
- extrinsics \(T^{cam}_c\)
- intrinsics \(\theta_c\) (fx, fy, cx, cy, distortion…)

---

## 2) Residual blocks (factor-graph view)

### A) Reprojection residuals (observations)
For each available DLC detection \((c,j,t)\):
- observed pixel \(u_{cjt}\)
- predicted pixel:
  \[
  \hat u_{cjt} = \pi\!\left(\theta_c,\; T^{cam}_c,\; X_{j,t}\right)
  \]

Residual:
\[
r_{obs} = W_{cjt}\,(\hat u_{cjt} - u_{cjt})
\]

Implementation details that matter:
- **Missing detection** → *no residual block*
- **Confidence weighting**: use DLC likelihood (or learned uncertainty) inside \(W_{cjt}\)
- **Robust loss** (Huber/Cauchy) to tolerate misdetections/outliers (Ceres supports this natively). citeturn0search1turn0search15

### B) Path constraint residuals (your key to fill gaps)
Add residuals between neighboring poses. Two common choices:

**Constant-velocity (1st order) in SE(3):**
- define relative motion:
  \[
  \Delta_t = T_t^{-1}T_{t+1}
  \]
- penalize changes in relative motion:
  \[
  r_{path}(t) = \mathrm{Log}(\Delta_{t}^{-1}\Delta_{t+1})
  \]
This enforces “smooth motion” without smoothing Euler angles.

**Constant-acceleration (2nd order)**
- translation second difference: \(p_{t+1}-2p_t+p_{t-1}\)
- rotation second difference via Lie algebra similarly

These factors are what keep \(T_t\) (and thus \(X_{j,t}\)) well-posed when 2D rays are missing.

### C) (Optional) Rigid-model “sanity” priors
If your rigid template is a crude approximation (it will be), you can add soft constraints that keep the optimization stable without over-constraining:
- keep certain “core” landmarks (torso/head anchor set) tighter
- allow others looser (paws, tail tip), by using different weights on their reprojection residuals

This is often more practical than adding many pairwise distance constraints if you already parameterize by a rigid template.

### D) Camera priors (when you enable camera refinement)
When estimating camera parameters, you *must* regularize:
\[
r_{cam} = (p - p_0)/\sigma
\]
for intrinsics and/or extrinsics, where \(p_0\) is calibration and \(\sigma\) is your trust.

Also fix gauge freedoms:
- keep one camera pose fixed (or enforce a world anchor), otherwise global drift/rotation can appear.

---

## 3) Initialization strategy (crucial for robustness)

Even with Ceres, you’ll want a good starting trajectory.

A practical rigid-stage initializer:
1. For frames where enough landmarks are visible in ≥2 cameras, triangulate a subset.
2. Estimate \(T_t\) by rigid alignment (Procrustes/Umeyama) from template points \(B_j\) to triangulated \(X_{j,t}\).
3. For frames with poor visibility, propagate from neighbors (constant velocity guess).
4. Then run the full optimization with robust losses.

This pairs nicely with your staged approach.

---

## 4) Why Ceres (backend) is the right tool here

Ceres is built exactly for “sum of residual blocks + robust loss + optional parameter constraints/manifolds,” and it’s widely used for BA-style problems. citeturn0search1turn0search7turn0search15

Two points specific to your model-based flavor:
- Your problem is *not* classic “camera + many free 3D points” BA; it’s closer to **pose trajectory estimation with reprojection factors** plus motion priors. The sparsity is still excellent, but Schur-elimination of points is less central.
- Solver choice: you’ll often do well with sparse normal Cholesky (and later iterative methods if scale grows). Ceres discusses solver choices for large sparse problems and BA settings. citeturn0search7turn0search9

---

## 5) Python front-end + Ceres back-end: practical options

### Option 1: Use an existing Ceres Python binding (fastest path)
- **pyceres (cvg/pyceres)** provides Python bindings + a factor-graph style interface aimed at BA / pose-graph problems. citeturn0search0turn0search2turn0search11  
This aligns well with your “residual blocks” design.

### Option 2: Build your own thin pybind11 wrapper (max control)
If you want full freedom in residual definitions/manifolds, a small custom C++ layer exposing:
- problem construction,
- custom cost functions,
- solve call,
- result extraction  
can be cleaner long-term.

There are example projects wrapping Ceres with pybind11 that even allow defining cost functions in Python (though performance/complexity tradeoffs apply). citeturn0search3turn0search8

**Recommendation:** start with pyceres if it covers your needs; otherwise move to a custom wrapper once residual complexity grows (especially when you add deformable latent states and learned priors).

---

## 6) Extending to deformable later (compatible by construction)

When you add deformable shape:
- add per-frame latent \(z_t\)
- define \(X_{j,t} = T_t(B_j + \Delta_j(z_t))\)
- add priors: \(\|z_t\|^2\) and temporal smoothness on \(z_t\)

Nothing else changes: reprojection residuals stay identical; path residuals extend naturally to include \(z_t\).

---

If you want to nail down the design next, the most consequential choice to specify (still “discussion-only”) is:

- which landmarks belong to the **rigid template core** (drive pose strongly),
- which landmarks are “non-rigid-ish” and should be **loosely** handled in the rigid stage,
- and which exact **path prior order** (velocity vs acceleration vs spline) you prefer as the default residual family.

Assistant: Good — we can now pin this down into a concrete, Ceres-centric design that matches your files and your spline idea, while keeping the “BA + constraints” interpretation.

## 1) Cameras and projection model (aligned with your `cameras.json`)
Your `cameras.json` already contains \(f_x,f_y,c_x,c_y\) plus \(R,t\) for each of the three cameras. fileciteturn1file6  
A key point: the `t` in your file is consistent with the standard form

\[
x_{cam} = R\,X_{world} + t,\quad \text{with } t = -R\,C
\]

because `pos_world` (camera center \(C\)) and `t` match that relationship (e.g., `cam1_top`). fileciteturn1file6

So for the simulated case (no distortion), your projection can be:

\[
u = f_x \frac{x}{z} + c_x,\quad v = f_y \frac{y}{z} + c_y
\]

Later, to support real data, add distortion parameters as optional extra intrinsics (and simply skip them when absent).

## 2) Rigid mouse template (your `.txt` model)
Your rigid template provides 10 body parts with local coordinates plus a skeletal edge list. fileciteturn3file1  
This naturally supports the model-based trajectory formulation:

\[
X_{j,t}^{world} = T_t \, B_j
\]

where \(B_j\) are your template coordinates and \(T_t\in SE(3)\) is the pose per frame (per mouse).

## 3) Observation residuals (2D reprojection) with missing data
You’re right: if a detection is missing, there is no observation equation → no residual block.

For each available DeepLabCut measurement \((cam=c,\; mouse=m,\; joint=j,\; time=t)\), add one residual:

\[
r_{obs} = \sqrt{w_{j}\,w_{conf}} \; \big(\pi_c(T_{m,t}B_j) - u_{c,m,j,t}\big)
\]

- \(w_j\) = your anatomical weight schedule (head/tail_root 1.0, ears 0.5, paws+nose 0.3, tail_tip 0.1).
- \(w_{conf}\) = DLC likelihood (or a monotone transform of it).
- Add a robust loss (Huber/Cauchy) to tolerate occasional mislabels.

This matches your desire that some joints should influence the pose strongly and others only weakly.

## 4) Path constraint: spline vs velocity/acceleration (they’re not contradictory)
You’re also right that “spline” and “velocity/acceleration” are not competing concepts — they’re different ways to express *smoothness*.

### Two clean ways to implement the spline idea in Ceres

#### A) Hard spline parameterization (true “condition equation” style)
Unknowns are **spline control points** (knots). The per-frame pose components are *not* free variables; they are computed from the spline:

- translation: cubic B-spline for \(p_t\)
- rotation: either
  - a spline in Lie algebra (spline over \(\log(R)\) increments), or
  - a quaternion spline (more work)

Then the only residuals are reprojection residuals (plus optional camera priors later). The “condition equation” \(p(t)=S(t)\) is enforced by construction.

**Pros:** strongest interpolation behavior, fewer parameters, excellent when occlusions are long.  
**Cons:** less flexible; if motion deviates from the spline model locally, it will “pull” the whole trajectory.

#### B) Soft spline prior (practically easier + safer initially)
Keep \(T_{m,t}\) as variables per frame, and add a spline “pseudo-observation”:

\[
r_{spline}(t) = \sqrt{\lambda}\,(p_t - S(t;\; \text{control points}))
\]

Optionally also for rotation.

This is exactly how constrained least-squares is typically implemented in modern solvers: the constraint is a weighted residual, not a separate Lagrange multiplier system.

**Pros:** robust and debuggable; lets observations override the spline locally.  
**Cons:** more parameters than hard spline; requires tuning \(\lambda\).

### Where velocity/acceleration fits
If you use a discrete acceleration prior:

\[
r_{acc}(t)=\sqrt{\lambda}\,(p_{t+1}-2p_t+p_{t-1})
\]

that is essentially a discrete analogue of penalizing curvature (second derivative), and *smoothing splines are strongly related to exactly this kind of penalty*. So you can start with velocity/acceleration residual blocks (cheap, stable), and later switch to explicit spline control points if you want stronger global interpolation.

## 5) Your concern: “rigid model spoils perfect observations”
This is an important subtlety, and you’re right to raise it.

In the model-based formulation, rigidity is *not* an extra term — it is the parameterization \(X_{j,t}=T_tB_j\). That means:

- If the world is truly rigid and observations are perfect → great.
- If observations are perfect but the animal is not rigid (it isn’t) → the optimizer must compromise, and the pose can shift to reduce the reprojection error of non-rigid joints.

Your weighting scheme is the correct *stage-1* mitigation: make pose be “owned” by joints that behave most rigidly/stably (head, tail_root), and let paws/tail_tip contribute weakly. fileciteturn3file1  
That way, “perfect” paw observations won’t torque the pose much, even if they disagree with the rigid template.

Two additional mitigation options (still compatible with “rigid-first”):

1) **Core-joints-only pose fit (inside the same solve):**  
   Use only head + tail_root (+ maybe ears) as high-weight pose drivers; everything else stays low-weight. This is basically what you already proposed.

2) **Tiny “slack offsets” (a bridge to deformable):**  
   Add per-joint offsets \(\delta_{j,t}\) but strongly regularize them:
   \[
   X_{j,t}=T_t(B_j+\delta_{j,t}),\quad r_\delta=\|\delta_{j,t}\|/\sigma
   \]
   With small \(\sigma\), you’re still “almost rigid,” but you avoid pose corruption when some joints are systematically non-rigid. This is often a more stable step before introducing a full deformable latent model.

## 6) Ceres backend: adapt your current residual code conceptually
You uploaded observation-equation examples that already use Ceres residual blocks (e.g. `ReprojectionErrorUsingP`, `UnknownPointReprojectionErrorUsingP`). fileciteturn1file2  
Two practical notes for the next iteration (conceptual, not coding yet):

- `ReprojectionErrorUsingP` uses **NumericDiff** and OpenCV matrices inside the cost function. fileciteturn1file2  
  That will become very slow at your scale (many frames × joints × cameras × two mice), and NumericDiff is fragile.

- For the production path, you’ll want **templated AutoDiff** cost functions using plain arrays, and use Ceres’ rotation utilities (`AngleAxisRotatePoint` or quaternion local parameterization). Your current `SnavelyReprojectionError` pattern shows exactly how that structure should look. fileciteturn1file9

So: your files are a good starting point structurally (residual-per-observation), but the next conceptual step is to re-express them in a high-throughput AutoDiff style.

## 7) Python front-end + pybind11 backend (your preferred Option 2)
This is reasonable, especially since you want tight control.

A good minimal API boundary is:

- Python side:
  - load `cameras.json` fileciteturn1file6
  - load template `.txt` fileciteturn3file1
  - load DLC CSV and build a sparse list of observations (skip missing)
  - build initial pose guesses from triangulatable frames
  - call `solve()`, then postprocess/export 3D trajectories for behavior inference

- C++/Ceres side:
  - store cameras, template, observation list
  - build `ceres::Problem`:
    - add pose parameter blocks per frame per mouse
    - add reprojection residuals
    - add path residuals (acceleration first; spline later)
  - run solve, return poses (and optionally derived 3D joints)

This keeps your experimentation flexible (Python) while the heavy lifting stays in Ceres.

## 8) Camera estimation option (later; keep architecture-ready)
For real data, you’ll want toggles:
- fixed cameras (default)
- refine only extrinsics
- refine selected intrinsics (+ distortion)

When enabled, add:
- strong priors to calibration values
- gauge fixing (e.g., keep one camera pose fixed)

Your `Simple_Bundle_Problem` already has the notion of “nparms per camera” and variants with fixed/unknown points/cameras, which fits the conceptual direction. fileciteturn1file0

### What I’d do next (discussion-level decision)
Given your spline preference, I’d start with:

1) **Soft acceleration residuals** (quickly stabilizes tracking, easy to tune)  
2) Then upgrade to **soft spline prior** (control points + residual to spline)  
3) Only if needed, move to **hard spline parameterization** (true condition-equation enforcement)

This sequence keeps early debugging sane while still arriving at your paper’s spline-centric concept.

Assistant: Below is a **concrete, implementable inventory** of (i) **parameter blocks** and (ii) **residual block types** for **3 cameras** and **2 mice**, using the **model-based trajectory** flavor (pose-per-frame), a **rigid template**, **tiny slack offsets**, and a **soft spline prior**.

I’ll assume the rigid template body parts are exactly those in your mouse model file (10 nodes). fileciteturn4file0

---

## Notation (sets)

- Cameras: \(c \in \{1,2,3\}\) (top/front/side as in `cameras.json`). fileciteturn4file2  
- Mice: \(m \in \{A,B\}\)
- Body parts: \(j \in \{1,\dots,10\}\) (head, nose_tip, …, right_hind_paw). fileciteturn4file0
- Frames: \(t \in \{0,\dots,T-1\}\)

Rigid template point for part \(j\): \(B_j\) (fixed). fileciteturn4file0

---

## 1) Parameter blocks

### 1.1 Cameras (shared across both mice)
Default: **fixed** from `cameras.json` (no optimization). fileciteturn4file2  
Optional later: **refine** selected parameters.

For each camera \(c\):

**Extrinsics (optional variable):**
- rotation \(q^{cam}_c\) (quaternion, 4)  *or* angle-axis (3)
- translation \(t^{cam}_c\) (3)

**Intrinsics:**
- simulated stage: \(f_x,f_y,c_x,c_y\) (4) fileciteturn4file2  
- real stage (optional extension): add distortion params, e.g. \(k_1,k_2,p_1,p_2,k_3\) (5)

So per camera (when fully enabled):  
- **Extrinsics:** 7 (quat+trans)  
- **Intrinsics:** 4 (+ distortion later)

---

### 1.2 Mouse trajectory (per mouse, per frame)
For each mouse \(m\) and frame \(t\):

**Pose \(T_{m,t}\in SE(3)\):**
- rotation quaternion \(q_{m,t}\) (4)
- translation \(p_{m,t}\) (3)

So: **7 parameters per (mouse, frame)**.

---

### 1.3 Tiny slack offsets (per mouse, per frame, per joint)
To avoid “rigid model spoils non-rigid joints” while still allowing paws/ears to help stabilize pose, use:

- \(\delta_{m,j,t} \in \mathbb{R}^3\) for *non-core joints*.

**Recommended partition**
- **Core (no slack; \(\delta=0\) fixed):** `head`, `tail_root` (strong anchors)
- **Slack-enabled:** all other 8 joints (`nose_tip`, ears, paws, `tail_tip`) from your template list fileciteturn4file0

So slack block size: **3 parameters per (mouse, joint, frame)** for slack-enabled joints.

Body point prediction becomes:
\[
X_{m,j,t} = T_{m,t}\,\big(B_j + \delta_{m,j,t}\big)
\]
(with \(\delta_{m,j,t}=0\) for core joints)

---

### 1.4 Soft spline control points (per mouse)
Soft spline prior means: poses remain per-frame variables, but we add residuals that pull them toward a spline.

For each mouse \(m\):

**Translation spline control points**
- \(P_{m,k}\in\mathbb{R}^3,\; k=1..K\) (knot count depends on spacing)

**Rotation spline control points** (choose one representation)
- Option R1 (recommended for implementation): control points in \(\mathfrak{so}(3)\): \(\phi_{m,k}\in\mathbb{R}^3\)
- Option R2: quaternion control points \(Q_{m,k}\in\mathbb{H}\) (harder evaluation; still doable)

So per mouse: **3K translation params + (3K or 4K) rotation spline params**.

---

## 2) Residual block types

### A) Reprojection residual (per observed 2D detection)
For each available observation \((c,m,j,t)\) (if missing: no block):

**Residual dimension:** 2 (pixel x,y)

**Parameters involved:**
- camera params for camera \(c\) (fixed or variable)
- pose \((q_{m,t}, p_{m,t})\)
- slack offset \(\delta_{m,j,t}\) (if slack-enabled joint; otherwise omitted/treated as zero)

**Weighting + robust loss:**
- weight = \(\sqrt{w_j \cdot w_{conf}}\)
- robust loss: **Huber or Cauchy** (you requested this)
- joint weights per your plan (e.g., head/tail_root 1.0, ears 0.5, paws+nose 0.3, tail_tip 0.1)

This is the “BA observation equation” part.

---

### B) Slack offset prior (keeps “tiny” tiny)
For each slack-enabled \((m,j,t)\):

**Residual dimension:** 3

\[
r_{\delta}(m,j,t) = \delta_{m,j,t}/\sigma_j
\]

- \(\sigma_j\) joint-dependent (e.g., paws smaller than tail_tip if you want tail more flexible already in rigid stage)

This is the key that lets you *use paws for orientation without letting them distort the pose*.

---

### C) Slack temporal smoothness (optional but strongly recommended)
For each slack-enabled \((m,j,t)\):

**Residual dimension:** 3 (first difference) or 3 (second difference)

Examples:
- velocity-style: \(\delta_{t+1}-\delta_t\)
- acceleration-style: \(\delta_{t+1}-2\delta_t+\delta_{t-1}\)

This stabilizes slack during occlusions (otherwise slack can “jump”).

---

### D) Soft spline prior on translation (path constraint)
For each \((m,t)\):

**Residual dimension:** 3  
**Parameters involved:**
- per-frame translation \(p_{m,t}\)
- translation control points \(\{P_{m,k}\}\)

\[
r_{spline,p}(m,t)=\sqrt{\lambda_p}\,(p_{m,t} - S_p(t;\{P_{m,k}\}))
\]

This is your “condition equation” implemented as a modern *soft constraint residual*.

---

### E) Soft spline prior on rotation (path constraint)
For each \((m,t)\):

**Residual dimension:** 3  
**Parameters involved:**
- per-frame rotation \(q_{m,t}\)
- rotation control points \(\{\phi_{m,k}\}\) (or quaternion control points)

Preferred residual form (manifold-friendly):
\[
r_{spline,R}(m,t)=\sqrt{\lambda_R}\;\mathrm{Log}\!\left(R_{spline}(t)^{-1}\,R(q_{m,t})\right)
\]

(returns a 3-vector in \(\mathfrak{so}(3)\))

---

### F) Camera priors (only when camera estimation is enabled)
For each camera parameter you allow to move:

**Intrinsics prior residuals** (dim = number of intrinsic params enabled)
\[
r_{K}(c)= (K_c - K_{c,0})/\sigma_K
\]

**Extrinsics prior residuals** (dim = 6 typically)
\[
r_{T}(c)=\mathrm{Log}(T_{c,0}^{-1}T_c)/\sigma_T
\]

This is what prevents cameras from drifting when real data gets messy.

---

### G) Gauge fixing (only needed when enabling camera estimation)
Not a residual per se; typically you do one (or both):
- set one camera extrinsic block constant, **or**
- put an extremely tight prior on one camera pose

This is to remove global gauge freedoms.

---

## Quick “wiring diagram” (what touches what)

- **Reprojection** couples: camera \(c\) ↔ pose \((m,t)\) ↔ slack \((m,j,t)\)
- **Spline priors** couple: pose \((m,t)\) ↔ spline control points (mouse \(m\))
- **Slack priors** couple: slack \((m,j,t)\) alone
- **Slack smoothness** couples: slack \((m,j,t)\) ↔ slack \((m,j,t\pm1)\)
- **Camera priors** couple: camera parameters alone

Two mice are independent **except** they share the same camera blocks.

---

If you want the next step (still no coding): I can propose **default numeric scales** for \(\sigma_j, \lambda_p, \lambda_R\), and a recommended **knot spacing** (frames-per-knot) that behaves well for typical mouse motion and typical occlusion durations.

As you seem to try to balance 4-vector quaternion style with rotations being element of so(3), it may seem good for this reason as well.)

Assistant: ### Default numeric scales (in your units)

From your inputs, both the **camera/world coordinates** (cage is 400×300×200) and the **mouse template coordinates** are in **millimeters**. fileciteturn4file2 fileciteturn4file0

So I’ll propose defaults in **px** (image) and **mm / rad** (3D).

## 1) Observation model: confidence weighting + robust loss

### 1.1 Map DLC likelihood → pixel sigma
A practical default is to convert likelihood \(\ell\in[0,1]\) to a **per-observation pixel stddev**:

- \(\sigma_{\min}=1.5\) px (near-perfect detection)
- \(\sigma_{\max}=8\) px (very uncertain but still usable)

Example mapping (smooth, monotone):
\[
\sigma_{pix}(\ell)=\sigma_{\min}+(\,1-\ell\,)^2(\sigma_{\max}-\sigma_{\min})
\]

Then the scalar weight factor entering the residual is:
\[
w_{conf} = 1/\sigma_{pix}(\ell)^2
\]

**Hard gating (recommended):** treat \(\ell < 0.05\) as “missing” (skip residual entirely), because those rows in your CSV behave like missing detections (NaN x/y, likelihood 0). fileciteturn1file8

### 1.2 Robust loss
- **Huber**: \(\delta = 3\) px  
- **Cauchy**: \(c = 2.5\) px  

Both are reasonable; Cauchy is more aggressive against outliers.

---

## 2) Joint weights (your schedule)
Keep exactly your plan (applies as a multiplier to \(w_{conf}\)):

- head, tail_root: \(w_j=1.0\)
- left/right ear tips: \(w_j=0.5\)
- nose_tip + all paws: \(w_j=0.3\)
- tail_tip: \(w_j=0.1\)

These joints exist in the template as given. fileciteturn4file0

---

## 3) “Tiny slack offsets” priors (mm)

Slack offsets are the bridge you want: paws/ears can help pose stability, but not dominate it.

### 3.1 Which joints get slack?
- **No slack (fixed \(\delta=0\))**: head, tail_root
- **Slack-enabled**: nose_tip, both ears, all paws, tail_tip (8 joints)

### 3.2 Default slack magnitude \(\sigma_{\delta,j}\) (per frame)
A good first set (in **mm**) that keeps slack “tiny” relative to your ~50–65 mm body scale fileciteturn4file0:

- ears: \(\sigma_{\delta}=1.5\) mm  
- nose_tip: \(\sigma_{\delta}=2.0\) mm  
- front paws: \(\sigma_{\delta}=2.5\) mm  
- hind paws: \(\sigma_{\delta}=3.0\) mm  
- tail_tip: \(\sigma_{\delta}=10.0\) mm  

Interpretation: paws/ears can deviate a few mm from the rigid template, but not “solve away” pose; tail tip is allowed more freedom already.

### 3.3 Slack temporal smoothness (per-frame)
Add a first-difference smoothness on slack:
\[
\delta_{t+1}-\delta_t
\]
with defaults:
- ears: \(\sigma_{\Delta\delta}=0.8\) mm/frame  
- nose_tip: \(\sigma_{\Delta\delta}=1.0\) mm/frame  
- paws: \(\sigma_{\Delta\delta}=1.2\) mm/frame  
- tail_tip: \(\sigma_{\Delta\delta}=3.0\) mm/frame  

This prevents slack “teleporting” during occlusions.

---

## 4) Soft spline priors (path constraint)

### 4.1 Knot spacing
Since your CSV doesn’t encode FPS explicitly, choose a **frame-based** default that works for typical mouse motion:

- **default knot interval:** **every 5 frames**  
- if you later know FPS, a nice rule is: knot_interval ≈ fps × 0.15 s, clamped to [3, 12].

This gives enough flexibility that normal motion is captured by the spline, so the per-frame pose doesn’t need to deviate a lot.

### 4.2 Translation spline prior strength
Residual: \(p_t - S_p(t)\)

- default translation spline stddev: **\(\sigma_{spline,p}=3\) mm**

Meaning: if observations are absent, the trajectory will typically stay within a few mm of the spline prediction; if observations are strong, they override locally.

### 4.3 Rotation spline prior strength
Residual: \(\log(R_{spline}(t)^{-1}R_t)\) (3-vector in \(\mathfrak{so}(3)\))

- default rotation spline stddev: **\(\sigma_{spline,R}=5^\circ \approx 0.087\) rad**

This is strong enough to carry orientation through occlusions, but not so strong it “irons out” genuine reorientations if the spline knots are moderately dense.

---

## 5) Optional camera refinement priors (for later real data)

When enabled, keep priors tight initially:

- extrinsic translation prior: \(\sigma_{cam,t} = 2\) mm  
- extrinsic rotation prior: \(\sigma_{cam,R} = 0.25^\circ \approx 0.0044\) rad  
- intrinsics:
  - \(f_x,f_y\): \(\sigma_f = 2\) px
  - \(c_x,c_y\): \(\sigma_c = 2\) px  
- distortion (if introduced): start very tight, then relax after validation.

Also: fix one camera pose (or very tight prior) to remove gauge freedom.

---

# Rodrigues (rotation vector) vs quaternion in this setup

If by “Rodrigues parameters” you mean the **rotation vector / axis–angle 3-vector** (a vector whose direction is the axis and whose norm is the angle), that is a perfectly usable **minimal** rotation parameterization. It’s directly tied to the Lie algebra and Rodrigues/exponential mapping. citeturn1search1turn1search0

**However:**
- The axis–angle / rotation-vector representation is **not unique** globally (wrap-around, ambiguity near \(\pi\), etc.), which can create discontinuities unless you actively keep angles in a consistent range. citeturn1search0
- In Ceres, the most “standard BA-like” practice is to **store quaternions** and use a **QuaternionManifold / EigenQuaternionManifold**, so updates happen in the correct 3D tangent space while maintaining unit norm automatically. citeturn1search5turn0search10

Given you already want spline priors expressed via \(\log(\cdot)\in\mathfrak{so}(3)\), you’ll be using a 3-vector Lie-algebra residual anyway. So the cleanest combination is:

- **Quaternion as the parameter block** (robust global representation in Ceres)
- **so(3) (rotation-vector) as the residual space** for your spline/relative-rotation constraints

That gives you the best of both: stable optimization + Lie-algebra-consistent smoothing.

If you *really* prefer Rodrigues as the stored parameter, I’d only do it if you also enforce a proper manifold “plus” (composition via exp) and handle wrap-around carefully; otherwise, additive updates in \(\mathbb{R}^3\) can behave poorly under large corrections.

## Coding started (Python front-end + pybind11 + Ceres backend)

I created a complete project skeleton implementing:

- Reprojection residuals with per-observation weighting + robust loss
- Per-frame pose per mouse (quaternion + translation)
- Per-joint per-frame slack offsets (with priors + smoothness)
- Soft cubic B-spline priors for translation and rotation (rotation prior implemented via exp/log on quaternions)
- Python utilities to load your `cameras.json`, `mouse3_mesh_shortTail.txt`, `dlc_long.csv`, and generate an initialization via multi-view triangulation + Umeyama alignment

You can download it here:

[Download mouse_ba_project.zip](sandbox:/mnt/data/mouse_ba_project.zip)

### Important note
This runtime environment doesn’t include Ceres headers/libraries, so I can’t compile/test the module *here*. The project is set up to build cleanly on a machine with **Ceres + Eigen + pybind11** installed.

If you want, next we can:
1) align the camera/world transform conventions with your existing C++ files *exactly* (one place this often bites), and

User: How do I have to compile (and use) the C/C++ code? I am asking for detailed hints as I never used a program that combines a python front-end with C/C++ running internally.

Assistant: What you’re building here is a **Python extension module**: C++ (using Ceres) is compiled into a shared library (`mouse_ba*.so` on Linux, `.pyd` on Windows, `.dylib/.so` on macOS). Python then does `import mouse_ba`, and calling `mouse_ba.solve(...)` jumps into the compiled C++ code.

Below is a practical, step-by-step way to compile and run it.

---

## 0) Unpack the project
```bash
unzip mouse_ba_project.zip
cd mouse_ba
```

You should see:
- `CMakeLists.txt`
- `src/mouse_ba_module.cpp` (pybind11 bindings)
- `python/example_run.py` (calls the module)
- `cameras.json`, `mouse3_mesh_shortTail.txt`, `dlc_long.csv`

---

## 1) Install prerequisites

### Option A (recommended on Ubuntu/Debian): system packages
```bash
sudo apt update
sudo apt install -y \
  build-essential cmake \
  python3-dev python3-pip \
  libeigen3-dev libceres-dev \
  pybind11-dev
```

Then Python deps:
```bash
python3 -m pip install --user numpy pandas
```

### Option B (cross-platform): conda-forge environment
This avoids “CMake can’t find Ceres” headaches.

```bash
conda create -n mouseba -c conda-forge python=3.11 ceres-solver eigen pybind11 cmake ninja numpy pandas
conda activate mouseba
```

---

## 2) Compile the C++ module with CMake

From the `mouse_ba/` folder:
```bash
mkdir -p build
cd build
cmake .. -DCMAKE_BUILD_TYPE=Release
cmake --build . -j
```

**Result:** you should get a file like:
- `build/mouse_ba.cpython-311-x86_64-linux-gnu.so` (name varies)

Quick sanity check:
```bash
python -c "import sys; sys.path.insert(0,'./'); import mouse_ba; print('mouse_ba imported OK')"
```

---

## 3) Run the Python example (and ensure Python finds the compiled module)

The example is in `mouse_ba/python/example_run.py` and it does `import mouse_ba`.

The compiled `.so` is in `mouse_ba/build/`, so you must add that to `PYTHONPATH`:

```bash
cd ../python
export PYTHONPATH=../build:$PYTHONPATH
python example_run.py
```

If it runs, you’ll see Ceres’ iteration summary printed (because `verbose=True` in the example).

---

## 4) Using it from your own Python scripts

In any script, ensure the compiled module directory is on the Python path:

```python
import sys
sys.path.insert(0, "/path/to/mouse_ba/build")

import mouse_ba
# then call mouse_ba.solve(...)
```

You’ll typically keep:
- all I/O + preprocessing in Python (loading CSV, building obs arrays, initialization),
- all optimization in C++ (Ceres).

---

## 5) Common build/runtime errors and fixes

### A) “CMake can’t find pybind11”
If you installed `pybind11` via pip instead of system/conda, point CMake to it:

```bash
python -m pybind11 --cmakedir
```

Then:
```bash
cmake .. -Dpybind11_DIR="$(python -m pybind11 --cmakedir)"
```

### B) “CMake can’t find Ceres”
If Ceres is installed in a non-standard prefix (common with conda), set:
```bash
cmake .. -DCMAKE_PREFIX_PATH="$CONDA_PREFIX" -DCMAKE_BUILD_TYPE=Release
```

Or explicitly:
```bash
cmake .. -DCeres_DIR=/path/to/CeresConfig.cmake
```

### C) ImportError: `libceres.so` not found (runtime linker problem)
This means the dynamic linker can’t find Ceres at runtime.

- If using system packages: usually not a problem.
- If using conda: ensure you’re in the conda env (`conda activate mouseba`).
- Otherwise:
```bash
export LD_LIBRARY_PATH=/path/to/ceres/lib:$LD_LIBRARY_PATH
```

### D) Wrong Python picked by CMake
Force it:
```bash
cmake .. -DPython_EXECUTABLE="$(which python)" -DCMAKE_BUILD_TYPE=Release
```

---

## 6) Mental model of “Python front-end + C++ backend”
- **Compilation time:** CMake builds `mouse_ba` as a shared library exposing functions to Python.
- **Runtime:** Python loads that `.so` just like a normal module.
- **Data passing:** you pass numpy arrays to `mouse_ba.solve(...)`; pybind11 converts them to C++ views; Ceres runs; results come back as numpy arrays / dict.

---

If you tell me your OS (Ubuntu/Windows/macOS, and whether you prefer conda), I can tailor the commands exactly (including the one-liner to build + run), but the steps above will work on a typical Linux machine as-is.

User: I have Ubuntu 22.04

Assistant: Great — Ubuntu 22.04 is a nice target for this. Here’s the most reliable build path, with “what to type” plus how to verify each step.

---

## 1) Install system dependencies (Ubuntu 22.04)

```bash
sudo apt update
sudo apt install -y \
  build-essential cmake pkg-config \
  python3-dev python3-pip \
  libeigen3-dev libceres-dev \
  pybind11-dev
```

Python packages used by the front-end:

```bash
python3 -m pip install --user -U numpy pandas
```

Sanity check:

```bash
cmake --version
python3 --version
dpkg -l | grep -E "libceres-dev|pybind11-dev|libeigen3-dev"
```

---

## 2) Unpack the project and build

```bash
unzip mouse_ba_project.zip
cd mouse_ba
mkdir -p build
cd build
cmake .. -DCMAKE_BUILD_TYPE=Release
cmake --build . -j
```

After `cmake --build`, you should see a compiled Python extension in `build/`, something like:

- `mouse_ba.cpython-310-x86_64-linux-gnu.so`

List it:

```bash
ls -lh *.so
```

---

## 3) Test import of the compiled module

Still inside `mouse_ba/build`:

```bash
python3 -c "import sys; sys.path.insert(0,'.'); import mouse_ba; print('OK: mouse_ba imported')"
```

If that prints the OK line, the C++/pybind11 side is working.

---

## 4) Run the example Python script

The example lives in `mouse_ba/python/`. It needs the compiled `.so` on `PYTHONPATH`.

```bash
cd ../python
export PYTHONPATH=../build:$PYTHONPATH
python3 example_run.py
```

If it’s wired correctly, you should see Ceres solver progress / summary printed (depending on verbosity).

---

## 5) How you use it in your own scripts

In any Python script, you can do either:

### Option A: set `PYTHONPATH` once in the terminal
```bash
export PYTHONPATH=/full/path/to/mouse_ba/build:$PYTHONPATH
python3 my_script.py
```

### Option B: insert it in code
```python
import sys
sys.path.insert(0, "/full/path/to/mouse_ba/build")
import mouse_ba
```

---

## 6) Common Ubuntu 22.04 gotchas (and fixes)

### A) CMake can’t find pybind11
This is rare if `pybind11-dev` is installed, but if it happens:

```bash
sudo apt install -y pybind11-dev
```

Then wipe the build folder and re-run cmake:

```bash
cd ../
rm -rf build
mkdir build && cd build
cmake .. -DCMAKE_BUILD_TYPE=Release
cmake --build . -j
```

### B) ImportError about missing `libceres.so`
On Ubuntu with `libceres-dev`, the runtime library is normally present. If you see:

> ImportError: libceres.so.X: cannot open shared object file

Install the runtime ceres library (usually already installed with `libceres-dev`, but ensure):

```bash
sudo apt install -y libceres2
sudo ldconfig
```

Check what your extension links against:

```bash
ldd mouse_ba*.so | grep -E "ceres|glog|gflags|suitesparse"
```

### C) Wrong Python version picked by CMake
Force it (Ubuntu 22.04 default is Python 3.10):

```bash
cmake .. -DCMAKE_BUILD_TYPE=Release -DPython_EXECUTABLE=/usr/bin/python3
```

---

## 7) Recommended workflow for development
When you edit C++:

```bash
cd mouse_ba/build
cmake --build . -j
```

Then re-run your Python script (no reinstall needed).

I can point out immediately if CMake is finding the right Python/Ceres, but you should be able to get it building on Ubuntu 22.04 with the steps above.

## Notes (conda specifics on Ubuntu)
- If you ever see link/runtime errors, ensure you *activated* the env (`conda activate mouseba`) before building/running.

User: Yes, please provide build.sh and run_example.sh

Assistant: from pathlib import Path

build_sh = r"""#!/usr/bin/env bash
set -euo pipefail

# Build the C++/pybind11 extension (Ceres backend) inside the active conda env.
# Usage:
#   conda activate mouseba
#   ./build.sh

if [[ -z "${CONDA_PREFIX:-}" ]]; then
  echo "ERROR: CONDA_PREFIX is not set. Activate your conda env first:"
  echo "  conda activate mouseba"
  exit 1
fi

# Resolve project root to the directory containing this script
ROOT_DIR="$(cd "$(dirname "${BASH_SOURCE[0]}")" && pwd)"
BUILD_DIR="${ROOT_DIR}/build"

mkdir -p "${BUILD_DIR}"
cd "${BUILD_DIR}"

echo "==> Configuring with CMake (prefix: ${CONDA_PREFIX})"
cmake .. \
  -DCMAKE_BUILD_TYPE=Release \
  -DCMAKE_PREFIX_PATH="${CONDA_PREFIX}" \
  -DPython_EXECUTABLE="$(command -v python)" \
  -GNinja

echo "==> Building"
cmake --build . -j

echo
echo "==> Build finished."
echo "    Extension should be in: ${BUILD_DIR}"
ls -1 "${BUILD_DIR}"/*.so 2>/dev/null || true
"""

run_example_sh = r"""#!/usr/bin/env bash
set -euo pipefail

# Run the Python example using the compiled extension.
# Usage:
#   conda activate mouseba
#   ./build.sh
#   ./run_example.sh

if [[ -z "${CONDA_PREFIX:-}" ]]; then
  echo "ERROR: CONDA_PREFIX is not set. Activate your conda env first:"
  echo "  conda activate mouseba"
  exit 1
fi

ROOT_DIR="$(cd "$(dirname "${BASH_SOURCE[0]}")" && pwd)"
BUILD_DIR="${ROOT_DIR}/build"
PY_DIR="${ROOT_DIR}/python"

# Ensure the module exists
if ! ls "${BUILD_DIR}"/mouse_ba*.so >/dev/null 2>&1; then
  echo "ERROR: Could not find compiled extension in ${BUILD_DIR}."
  echo "Run ./build.sh first."
  exit 1
fi

export PYTHONPATH="${BUILD_DIR}:${PYTHONPATH:-}"

cd "${PY_DIR}"
echo "==> Running example_run.py"
python example_run.py
"""

Path("/mnt/data/build.sh").write_text(build_sh)
Path("/mnt/data/run_example.sh").write_text(run_example_sh)

# Make them executable (best effort)
import os, stat
for p in ["/mnt/data/build.sh", "/mnt/data/run_example.sh"]:
    st = os.stat(p)
    os.chmod(p, st.st_mode | stat.S_IEXEC)

("/mnt/data/build.sh", "/mnt/data/run_example.sh")

Assistant: You’re extremely close — the build is failing for a *CMake language-configuration reason*, not because OpenMP is actually missing on your machine.

### Why this happens
`find_package(Ceres)` pulls in SuiteSparse, and SuiteSparse’s config calls `find_dependency(OpenMP)` without restricting languages. CMake then tries to find **OpenMP for C and C++**. In your project, the top-level CMakeLists enables **only CXX**:

```cmake
project(mouse_ba LANGUAGES CXX)
```

So CMake never configures a C compiler → it can’t satisfy `OpenMP_C_FOUND`, which is exactly what your error says. This is a known behavior: FindOpenMP needs the corresponding language enabled in the project. citeturn0search1turn0search14

---

## Fix (recommended): enable C language in your project

### 1) Edit `CMakeLists.txt`
Change this line:

```cmake
project(mouse_ba LANGUAGES CXX)
```

to:

```cmake
project(mouse_ba LANGUAGES C CXX)
```

You can do it with one command:

```bash
sed -i 's/project(mouse_ba LANGUAGES CXX)/project(mouse_ba LANGUAGES C CXX)/' CMakeLists.txt
```

### 2) Clean and rebuild
```bash
rm -rf build
./build.sh
```

That should make CMake detect the conda C compiler and then OpenMP.

---

## If it *still* complains about OpenMP
In a conda-forge toolchain, the OpenMP runtime is typically **libgomp** (GCC’s OpenMP library). citeturn1search0turn1search4  
Install/ensure it explicitly:

```bash
conda install -n mouseba -c conda-forge libgomp
```

Then rebuild again (`rm -rf build && ./build.sh`).

---

## About the CMP0148 warning (pybind11 + CMake 4.x)
That warning is harmless; it won’t break your build. If you want it quiet, add this *before* `find_package(pybind11 REQUIRED)`:

```cmake
set(PYBIND11_FINDPYTHON ON)
```

(You can ignore it for now.)

---

## One small improvement to `build.sh` (optional, but helps determinism)
If you want to be extra explicit, you can pass the conda compilers to CMake:

```bash
-DCMAKE_C_COMPILER="$(command -v x86_64-conda-linux-gnu-cc)" \
-DCMAKE_CXX_COMPILER="$(command -v x86_64-conda-linux-gnu-c++)" \
```

But in most conda envs, once you enable `LANGUAGES C CXX`, it “just works.”

---

If you apply the one-line CMakeLists change and re-run, and it still fails, paste the new CMake output from `./build.sh` and I’ll tell you exactly which dependency is missing next.

Assistant: Perfect — the CMake/OpenMP issue is solved. Now you’re hitting two **real compile errors** in the C++ code:

1) **pybind11 dict iteration:** `item.first` / `item.second` are `py::handle`, and `py::int_(handle)` / `py::float_(handle)` doesn’t compile with your pybind11 (3.0.2).

2) **AutoDiff + Jet types:** your `so3.h` uses `std::sqrt/sin/cos/atan2`. Those don’t work for `ceres::Jet<>`. You must use **Ceres’ Jet-friendly math / rotation utilities**.

Below are the exact fixes.

---

## A) Fix pybind11 conversions in `src/mouse_ba_module.cpp`

Find both places where you iterate over a Python dict:

### 1) In `joint_weight` dict loop, replace:
```cpp
int j = py::int_(item.first);
double w = py::float_(item.second);
```

with:
```cpp
int j = py::cast<int>(item.first);
double w = py::cast<double>(item.second);
```

### 2) In `read_map(...)` lambda, replace:
```cpp
int j = py::int_(item.first);
double v = py::float_(item.second);
```

with:
```cpp
int j = py::cast<int>(item.first);
double v = py::cast<double>(item.second);
```

That resolves the `no matching function for pybind11::int_(handle&)` error.

---

## B) Fix Jet-compatible trig/sqrt in `src/so3.h`

Your errors like:

- `no matching function for call to sqrt(ceres::Jet<...>)`
- `no matching function for call to sin(ceres::Jet<...>)`
- `atan2(...)` etc.

come from using `std::sqrt/sin/cos/atan2` in templated functions evaluated with `ceres::Jet`.

### Best fix: use Ceres rotation functions

Change `so3.h` to use:

- `ceres::AngleAxisToQuaternion` (exp map)
- `ceres::QuaternionToAngleAxis` (log map)
- and use `ceres::sqrt` for quaternion normalization

Do this:

1) At top of `so3.h`, replace `#include <cmath>` with:
```cpp
#include <ceres/rotation.h>
#include <ceres/jet.h>
```

2) In `quat_normalize`, replace `std::sqrt` with `ceres::sqrt`:
```cpp
const T n = ceres::sqrt(q[0]*q[0] + q[1]*q[1] + q[2]*q[2] + q[3]*q[3]);
```

3) Replace your entire `so3_exp` body with:
```cpp
ceres::AngleAxisToQuaternion(w, q);
```

4) Replace your entire `so3_log` body with:
```cpp
ceres::QuaternionToAngleAxis(q_in, w);
```

This removes all Jet-incompatible math and is the standard robust way.

---

## C) (If not already) enable C in `CMakeLists.txt`

You already did this (your output shows a C compiler was detected), but just to be explicit:

```cmake
project(mouse_ba LANGUAGES C CXX)
```

Optional (silences the CMP0148 warning):
add before `find_package(pybind11 REQUIRED)`:
```cmake
set(PYBIND11_FINDPYTHON ON)
```

---

## D) Rebuild cleanly

Because you changed headers and module code, do a clean rebuild:

```bash
rm -rf build
./build.sh
```

Then run:

```bash
./run_example.sh
```

---

## If you prefer: I can give you a ready-to-unzip fixed project
I produced a corrected archive with the changes above:

[Download mouse_ba_project_fixed.zip](sandbox:/mnt/data/mouse_ba_project_fixed.zip)

You can either:
- unzip it into a new folder and build there, or
- just copy these files into your existing repo:
  - `src/mouse_ba_module.cpp`
  - `src/so3.h`
  - (optionally) `CMakeLists.txt`

./run_example.sh: line 31: 16627 Segmentation fault      (core dumped) python example_run.py

Assistant: Nice — you’ve now got a **real runtime crash**, not a build issue.

### Why the segfault happens (almost certainly)
In your current `mouse_ba_module.cpp` you keep **Manifold objects** (and the **LossFunction**) in `std::unique_ptr`, *and* you also hand their raw pointers to `ceres::Problem`. But **Ceres `Problem` takes ownership of the `Manifold` and `LossFunction` pointers by default** and deletes them when the `Problem` is destroyed. If you also delete them via `unique_ptr`, you get a **double-free** → segfault right after the solver finishes printing iterations (exactly what you see). citeturn0search3

So: remove your ownership, or tell `Problem` not to own them.

---

## Fix (recommended): let Ceres own the Manifold/LossFunction

### 1) Patch `make_loss` to return a raw pointer (no `unique_ptr`)
In `src/mouse_ba_module.cpp`, replace:

```cpp
static std::unique_ptr<ceres::LossFunction> make_loss(const WeightPack& wp) { ... }
```

with:

```cpp
static ceres::LossFunction* make_loss(const WeightPack& wp) {
  if (wp.robust_loss == "none") return nullptr;
  if (wp.robust_loss == "huber") return new ceres::HuberLoss(wp.robust_param_px);
  if (wp.robust_loss == "cauchy") return new ceres::CauchyLoss(wp.robust_param_px);
  throw std::runtime_error("Unknown robust_loss: " + wp.robust_loss);
}
```

Then in `solve(...)`, replace:

```cpp
auto loss = make_loss(wp);
...
problem.AddResidualBlock(cf, loss.get(), ...);
```

with:

```cpp
ceres::LossFunction* loss = make_loss(wp);
...
problem.AddResidualBlock(cf, loss, ...);
```

Do **not** delete `loss` yourself—Ceres will. citeturn0search3

---

### 2) Remove the `std::vector<std::unique_ptr<ceres::Manifold>> manifolds;`
Right now you have:

```cpp
std::vector<std::unique_ptr<ceres::Manifold>> manifolds;
auto make_quat_manifold = [&]() { ... return manifolds.back().get(); };
...
problem.AddParameterBlock(q, 4, make_quat_manifold());
```

Replace that whole manifold machinery with **one heap manifold** reused everywhere:

```cpp
#if CERES_VERSION_MAJOR >= 2
ceres::Manifold* quat_manifold = new ceres::EigenQuaternionManifold();
#else
ceres::LocalParameterization* quat_manifold = new ceres::QuaternionParameterization();
#endif
```

and then use `quat_manifold` wherever you currently call `make_quat_manifold()`:

```cpp
problem.AddParameterBlock(q, 4, quat_manifold);
```

Again: do **not** delete it; Ceres will delete it exactly once even if many parameter blocks reference it. citeturn0search3

---

## Rebuild cleanly
```bash
rm -rf build
./build.sh
./run_example.sh
```

This should eliminate the segfault.

---

## If you want to confirm with a backtrace (optional)
If you’re curious, this kind of crash typically shows up as `free(): invalid pointer` / double free in a backtrace.

```bash
ulimit -c unlimited
./run_example.sh
# if it crashes, you get a core file: core.<pid>
gdb -q $(which python) core*
(gdb) bt
```

You’ll usually see it dying during teardown/destructors.

If you paste your current `src/mouse_ba_module.cpp` (or just the parts around the manifold/loss creation), I can tell you exactly what lines to change. But the patch above matches your symptoms precisely.

static ceres::LossFunction* make_loss(const WeightPack& wp) {
  // NOTE: By default, ceres::Problem takes ownership of LossFunction pointers.
  // We intentionally return a raw pointer allocated with new and do NOT delete it.
  // Ceres will delete it exactly once (sharing is allowed).
  if (wp.robust_loss == "none") return nullptr;
  if (wp.robust_loss == "huber") return new ceres::HuberLoss(wp.robust_param_px);
  if (wp.robust_loss == "cauchy") return new ceres::CauchyLoss(wp.robust_param_px);

- `AddResidualBlock(..., loss.get(), ...)` became `AddResidualBlock(..., loss, ...)`.

Assistant: from pathlib import Path
import textwrap, os, stat

code = r"""#!/usr/bin/env python3
"""
code += r'''
"""
baWithPathModel.py

CLI wrapper around the Ceres backend (mouse_ba) using a Python front-end.
- Reads cameras.json, mouse template (.txt), and dlc_long.csv
- Initializes poses from multi-view triangulation
- Runs model-based BA with soft spline (path) priors + tiny slack offsets
- Writes estimated 3D body-part coordinates in coords_3d.csv format
- Optionally compares against ground-truth coords_3d.csv and prints accuracy stats

Place this script in the `python/` folder (next to frontend.py), or adjust imports accordingly.
"""

from __future__ import annotations

import argparse
import sys
from pathlib import Path
from typing import Dict, Tuple, List, Optional

import numpy as np
import pandas as pd


def _auto_add_build_to_syspath() -> None:
    """
    Make 'import mouse_ba' work without requiring the user to set PYTHONPATH.

    Expected layout:
      project_root/
        build/  (contains mouse_ba*.so)
        python/
          baWithPathModel.py   <-- this script
          frontend.py
    """
    script_dir = Path(__file__).resolve().parent
    build_dir = script_dir.parent / "build"
    if build_dir.exists():
        sys.path.insert(0, str(build_dir))


_auto_add_build_to_syspath()

# Local front-end helpers (from your project)
from frontend import (
    load_cameras_json,
    load_dlc_long_csv,
    init_poses_from_triangulation,
    default_weight_pack,
)

import mouse_ba


# -----------------------------
# Parsing template (names + pts)
# -----------------------------
def load_template_names_points(path: str | Path) -> Tuple[List[str], np.ndarray]:
    """
    Reads mouse3_mesh_shortTail.txt-like file.

    Expected first block: lines
      <name> <x> <y> <z>
    until a blank line, then skeleton edges.

    Returns:
      names: list[str] length J
      pts: (J,3) float64
    """
    path = Path(path)
    names: List[str] = []
    pts: List[List[float]] = []
    with path.open("r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        for line in f:
            s = line.strip()
            if not s:
                break
            parts = s.split()
            if len(parts) != 4:
                break
            name = parts[0]
            try:
                x, y, z = float(parts[1]), float(parts[2]), float(parts[3])
            except ValueError:
                break
            names.append(name)
            pts.append([x, y, z])
    if not names:
        raise ValueError(f"Template file {path} did not contain a '<name> x y z' block.")
    return names, np.asarray(pts, dtype=np.float64)


# -----------------------------
# Quaternion helpers (w,x,y,z)
# -----------------------------
def quat_normalize(q: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    n = np.linalg.norm(q, axis=-1, keepdims=True)
    n = np.where(n == 0.0, 1.0, n)
    return q / n


def quat_to_rotmat_wxyz(q: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    q: (...,4) in (w,x,y,z) order.
    Returns: (...,3,3)
    """
    q = quat_normalize(q)
    w, x, y, z = q[..., 0], q[..., 1], q[..., 2], q[..., 3]
    ww, xx, yy, zz = w*w, x*x, y*y, z*z

    wx, wy, wz = w*x, w*y, w*z
    xy, xz, yz = x*y, x*z, y*z

    R = np.empty(q.shape[:-1] + (3, 3), dtype=np.float64)
    R[..., 0, 0] = ww + xx - yy - zz
    R[..., 0, 1] = 2.0*(xy - wz)
    R[..., 0, 2] = 2.0*(xz + wy)

    R[..., 1, 0] = 2.0*(xy + wz)
    R[..., 1, 1] = ww - xx + yy - zz
    R[..., 1, 2] = 2.0*(yz - wx)

    R[..., 2, 0] = 2.0*(xz - wy)
    R[..., 2, 1] = 2.0*(yz + wx)
    R[..., 2, 2] = ww - xx - yy + zz
    return R


# -----------------------------
# Estimation -> coords_3d.csv
# -----------------------------
def build_estimated_coords_df(
    joint_names: List[str],
    template_pts: np.ndarray,           # (J,3)
    mouse_quat: np.ndarray,             # (M,T,4)
    mouse_trans: np.ndarray,            # (M,T,3)
    slack: np.ndarray,                  # (M,T,J,3)
    fps: float,
    behavior_default: int = 0,
) -> pd.DataFrame:
    """
    Produces DataFrame with columns:
      frame, time, mouse_id, behavior, node, x, y, z
    matching coords_3d.csv format.
    """
    M, T, J, _ = slack.shape
    assert template_pts.shape == (J, 3)

    R = quat_to_rotmat_wxyz(mouse_quat)   # (M,T,3,3)

    # local points (M,T,J,3)
    local = template_pts[None, None, :, :] + slack
    # world points: R @ local + t
    world = (R[..., None, :, :] @ local[..., None]).squeeze(-1) + mouse_trans[..., None, :]

    frames = np.arange(T, dtype=np.int32)
    times = frames.astype(np.float64) / float(fps)

    # Build row-wise records
    recs = []
    for m in range(M):
        for t in range(T):
            for j in range(J):
                recs.append((
                    int(frames[t]),
                    float(times[t]),
                    int(m),
                    int(behavior_default),
                    joint_names[j],
                    float(world[m, t, j, 0]),
                    float(world[m, t, j, 1]),
                    float(world[m, t, j, 2]),
                ))
    df = pd.DataFrame(
        recs,
        columns=["frame", "time", "mouse_id", "behavior", "node", "x", "y", "z"],
    )
    return df


def compare_to_ground_truth(est_df: pd.DataFrame, gt_path: str | Path) -> Dict[str, pd.DataFrame]:
    """
    Compares estimated coords against ground truth coords_3d.csv.
    Returns tables in a dict (overall + per-node + per-mouse).
    """
    gt_df = pd.read_csv(gt_path)
    required = {"frame", "mouse_id", "node", "x", "y", "z"}
    missing = required - set(gt_df.columns)
    if missing:
        raise ValueError(f"Ground truth file missing required columns: {sorted(missing)}")

    # Merge on keys
    keys = ["frame", "mouse_id", "node"]
    m = est_df.merge(gt_df[keys + ["x", "y", "z"]], on=keys, suffixes=("_est", "_gt"), how="inner")

    if len(m) == 0:
        raise ValueError("No matching rows between estimate and ground truth (check node names / frame count).")

    dx = m["x_est"].to_numpy() - m["x_gt"].to_numpy()
    dy = m["y_est"].to_numpy() - m["y_gt"].to_numpy()
    dz = m["z_est"].to_numpy() - m["z_gt"].to_numpy()
    err = np.sqrt(dx*dx + dy*dy + dz*dz)  # Euclidean error in mm
    m["err_mm"] = err

    def overall_table() -> pd.DataFrame:
        rmse = float(np.sqrt(np.mean(err**2)))
        mae = float(np.mean(np.abs(err)))
        med = float(np.median(err))
        p95 = float(np.percentile(err, 95))
        p99 = float(np.percentile(err, 99))
        return pd.DataFrame([{
            "N": len(err),
            "RMSE_mm": rmse,
            "MAE_mm": mae,
            "Median_mm": med,
            "P95_mm": p95,
            "P99_mm": p99,
        }])

    per_node = (
        m.groupby("node")["err_mm"]
         .agg(N="count", RMSE_mm=lambda x: float(np.sqrt(np.mean(np.square(x)))), MAE_mm="mean", Median_mm="median", P95_mm=lambda x: float(np.percentile(x, 95)))
         .reset_index()
         .sort_values("RMSE_mm", ascending=False)
    )

    per_mouse = (
        m.groupby("mouse_id")["err_mm"]
         .agg(N="count", RMSE_mm=lambda x: float(np.sqrt(np.mean(np.square(x)))), MAE_mm="mean", Median_mm="median", P95_mm=lambda x: float(np.percentile(x, 95)))
         .reset_index()
         .sort_values("mouse_id")
    )

    return {
        "overall": overall_table(),
        "per_node": per_node,
        "per_mouse": per_mouse,
        "merged": m,
    }


# -----------------------------
# CLI + main
# -----------------------------
def build_arg_parser() -> argparse.ArgumentParser:
    p = argparse.ArgumentParser(
        prog="baWithPathModel.py",
        description="Model-based bundle adjustment with soft spline (path) priors + rigid template + tiny slack offsets.",
    )
    p.add_argument("--cameras", required=True, help="Path to cameras.json")
    p.add_argument("--template", required=True, help="Path to mouse template file (e.g., mouse3_mesh_shortTail.txt)")
    p.add_argument("--dlc", required=True, help="Path to DLC long-format CSV (dlc_long.csv)")
    p.add_argument("--fps", type=float, default=10.0, help="Frames per second (default: 10)")
    p.add_argument("--out", required=True, help="Output CSV path for estimated 3D coordinates (coords_3d.csv format)")
    p.add_argument("--gt", default=None, help="Optional ground-truth coords_3d.csv to compare against")

    # Initialization controls
    p.add_argument("--init-likelihood-min", type=float, default=0.2,
                   help="Likelihood threshold used for triangulation-based initialization (default: 0.2)")

    # Solver controls
    p.add_argument("--max-iter", type=int, default=200, help="Ceres max iterations (default: 200)")
    p.add_argument("--threads", type=int, default=8, help="Ceres threads (default: 8)")
    p.add_argument("--verbose", action="store_true", help="Print Ceres iteration table")
    p.add_argument("--optimize-cameras", action="store_true", help="Also estimate camera extrinsics (default off)")
    p.add_argument("--optimize-intrinsics", action="store_true", help="Also estimate camera intrinsics (default off)")

    # Ablation-ish toggles (no backend changes required)
    p.add_argument("--disable-path", action="store_true",
                   help="Disable spline/path priors by setting their weights ~0 (useful for ablation).")
    p.add_argument("--rigid-only", action="store_true",
                   help="Force slack offsets ~0 by making sigma_delta extremely small (useful for ablation).")

    return p


def main() -> int:
    args = build_arg_parser().parse_args()

    cam_quat, cam_trans, cam_intr = load_cameras_json(args.cameras)
    joint_names, template_pts = load_template_names_points(args.template)

    (obs_cam, obs_mouse, obs_joint, obs_frame,
     obs_x, obs_y, obs_l, num_frames) = load_dlc_long_csv(args.dlc)

    init_q, init_t = init_poses_from_triangulation(
        template_pts,
        obs_cam, obs_mouse, obs_joint, obs_frame, obs_x, obs_y, obs_l,
        cam_quat, cam_trans, cam_intr,
        likelihood_min=args.init_likelihood_min,
    )

    # Weight pack defaults, then adjust for FPS + ablations.
    wp = default_weight_pack().to_dict()

    # Knot interval: default ≈ 0.3s worth of frames, min 3
    knot_interval = max(3, int(round(args.fps * 0.3)))
    wp["knot_interval_frames"] = knot_interval

    if args.disable_path:
        wp["sigma_spline_p_mm"] = 1e9
        wp["sigma_spline_R_rad"] = 1e9

    if args.rigid_only:
        # force slack to ~0 by extremely tight sigma
        # (core joints already fixed in C++; this clamps the remaining joints)
        tiny = 1e-6
        # Build per-joint dict by index
        sigma_delta = {j: tiny for j in range(len(joint_names))}
        sigma_deltadot = {j: tiny for j in range(len(joint_names))}
        wp["sigma_delta_mm"] = sigma_delta
        wp["sigma_deltadot_mm_per_frame"] = sigma_deltadot

    solver_opts = {
        "max_num_iterations": int(args.max_iter),
        "num_threads": int(args.threads),
        "verbose": bool(args.verbose),
        "optimize_cameras": bool(args.optimize_cameras),
        "optimize_intrinsics": bool(args.optimize_intrinsics),
    }

    out = mouse_ba.solve(
        cam_quat, cam_trans, cam_intr,
        template_pts,
        obs_cam, obs_mouse, obs_joint, obs_frame,
        obs_x, obs_y, obs_l,
        int(num_frames),
        init_q, init_t,
        wp,
        solver_opts,
    )

    print(out["summary"])

    est_df = build_estimated_coords_df(
        joint_names=joint_names,
        template_pts=template_pts,
        mouse_quat=np.asarray(out["mouse_quat"]),
        mouse_trans=np.asarray(out["mouse_trans"]),
        slack=np.asarray(out["slack"]),
        fps=float(args.fps),
        behavior_default=0,
    )

    out_path = Path(args.out)
    out_path.parent.mkdir(parents=True, exist_ok=True)
    est_df.to_csv(out_path, index=False)
    print(f"Wrote estimated 3D coordinates to: {out_path}")

    if args.gt is not None:
        stats = compare_to_ground_truth(est_df, args.gt)
        print("\n=== Accuracy vs ground truth (mm) ===")
        print(stats["overall"].to_string(index=False))
        print("\nPer-mouse:")
        print(stats["per_mouse"].to_string(index=False))
        print("\nPer-node (worst first):")
        print(stats["per_node"].to_string(index=False))

        # also report match coverage
        gt_df = pd.read_csv(args.gt)
        matched = len(stats["merged"])
        total_est = len(est_df)
        total_gt = len(gt_df)
        print(f"\nMatched rows: {matched} (est={total_est}, gt={total_gt})")

    return 0


if __name__ == "__main__":
    raise SystemExit(main())

Assistant: from pathlib import Path
